  <raw>US drug sales growth slows to 1.3%



Annual US prescription sales growth was just 1.3% in 2008, reaching $291.5 billion, according to IMS Health, a leading provider of market intelligence to the pharmaceutical and health care industries. Dispensed prescription volume in the USA grew at a 0.9% pace, it added. 

Factors influencing the market's slower growth in 2008 included higher demand for less-expensive generic medicines, lower new product sales and reduced consumer demand due to the economic turndown.
Across the major therapeutic classes, the biggest sales came from antipsychotics, increasing 11.5% to $14.6 billion. Lipid regulators, or cholesterol-lowerers, were the most widely-dispensed retail prescription medications on a volume basis in 2008, but second by value reaching a total of $14.6 billion, down 11.6% on 2007. This was followed by proton pump inhibitors at $13.9 billion, seeing a decline of 2.1%, and drugs for seizure disorders, totaling $1.3 billion, a rise of 10.8%.
In terms of individual drugs, Pfizer's mega block buster statin Lipitor (atorvastatin) generated the highest turnover of $7.8 billion, but down year-on-year by 3.7%. Next were: AstraZeneca's PPI Nexium (esomeprazole), at $5.9 billion and 7.3% higher than in 2007; and Sanofi-Aventis and Bristol-Myers Squibb's blood thinner Plavix, at $4.9 billion and leaping 26.4%. Biotechnology-based medicines accounted for 15%, or $45.9 billion, of the total US market, IMS said.
Cost sensitivity on part of consumers
"It is the lowest growth we have ever seen," said Diana Conmy, corporate director of market insights for IMS. "Patients are going to try to hang on to their prescription medicines as much as they can, but in some cases they have to make decisions on taking on a new therapy or moving from a branded to a generic and there is definitely a cost sensitivity," she added.
Ms Conmy also noted that the slowdown in demand has been worse during this current recession than those in the past because the cost of insurance premiums and co-payments have risen, putting more pressure on patients.</raw>
